Chapter III.
On restoration of the nose when broken.
Know that the nose is not broken into one piece but two pieces lifted up or one of them is observed because in it there are two bones, where the lower part is cartilaginous and is not actually broken but is bent, twisted, and swollen.
However, if one of the two parts of it is broken, then you must insert a small probe into its nose and guide that fracture inside with your finger and outside with a spoon handle until the nose returns to its natural shape. Make sure it is done easily, and be careful that your procedure does not harm the patient.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and does not extend to that, then it is necessary to align it with the end of the bone where there is a slight bulge.
If the fracture is in two parts, you should do the same thing.
Take care of it in the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then wait for the seventh or tenth day for the cauterization of the wound to heal.
Insert a piece of cloth into the end of the nose if the fracture is in one part, or insert two calipers if the fracture is in two parts, and make sure they are as thick as to fill the hole in the nose.
As some of the ancient restorers did, calipers should be wrapped in bandages and left on all day, but I have not seen it; however, it is necessary that the cloth should be wrapped in alum-oil emulsion with powdered millstone, and leave it in until the bone has set and the cartilage hardened.
And sometimes it should be inserted in the nose, in place of the nostril, for fear that a foreign body has penetrated it, wrapped in lion cloth; and if the patient's nose fracture is visible and obvious, and they are not forbidden to breathe.
This phrase does not concern you; if it pleases you, do this, and if it pleases you, perform cauterization. (Note: "cauteras de punt" refers to a heated metal probe used for cauterizing wounds.)
And if another boil appears, cauterize the nose with a horn or a piece of cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil, or with alum. (Note: alum is a mineral used as an astringent.)
But if the boil does not appear, then apply powder of chalk and cinnamon with alum on it, place your hand over it (the nose), and do not let anything come out from the nose.
But if the bones of the nose are fractured and small or comminuted (crushed into many pieces), then you should break them apart and remove them with instruments suitable for the case; and sew up the wound, and care for it with a dressing that promotes healing and consolidation, and with appropriate ointments.
If a plague occurs inside the nose, then you should care for it with warm compresses and administer lead tubes until it is completely healed. (Note: "canulas de plom" refers to lead tubes used to drain pus.)
Chapter IV. (Note: in medieval manuscripts, each chapter was often preceded by its number, usually in Roman numerals.)
On the restoration of the jawbone when it is fractured. (Note: "mandibula" refers to the lower jawbone.)
When the jawbone is broken and the fracture is not associated with a plague, consider first whether the fracture is only on the outside, and if it is not broken in two and is curved at the edges inside, then its diagnosis is easy. (Note: "plagua" refers to an infection or ulcer.)
Therefore, if the fracture is on the right side of the jawbone, you should insert your index finger (left hand) into the patient's mouth; and if the fracture is on the left jawbone, you should insert the index finger of your right hand; and you should press gently inward at the inner part of the fracture, while applying pressure outward on the edges of the bone with your other hand; doing this with care and evenly pressing both hands.
If the jawbone fracture has already separated into two parts, then you should administer extension of both parts so that their alignment can be achieved as much as possible.
If there is commotion of teeth to disruption (loose teeth), you should bind those that you hope will remain with gold or silver wire or gut; and place cotton over the fractured jawbone, and a double layer of cloth, and a broad strip of well-made cloth, or a single piece of gauze the length of the jawbone; and tie it up as necessary for the healing process, so that it is not destroyed. (Note: "cot" refers to cotton.)
And tell the patient to rest and remain calm, and restrict their intake of sour foods (lemon).
And if you suspect that the figure has been altered in some way, then examine it and loosen it on the third day; and correct any deformity with powder of milled rice, alum, or chalk flour (calcium carbonate); as you have removed the cotton, place wool over the dressing. (Note: "terra" refers to clay.)
And thus, when the dressing has been applied and the bone alignment is not altered, leave it in place until it is healed and strengthened (the fracture), as this fracture often takes three weeks to heal.
If another boil appears again, administer the same treatment as previously described until it is healed and the boil is drained. (Note: "remaugut" refers to drainage.)
If the fracture is associated with a plague, consider whether it is a bone fragment separated or osteitis (inflammation of the bone), and carefully remove those fragments with instruments suitable for the removal of such fragments.
If the orifices of the plague are narrow, you should enlarge them with a leeches (medieval medical instrument used for bloodletting) according to your need.
And when you have removed the fragments, and there is no more pus draining from them, you should sew up the orifices of the plague if they are wide; otherwise, cover it with one of the dressings suitable for that purpose and let it heal.
Chapter V. (Note: in medieval manuscripts, each chapter was often preceded by its number, usually in Roman numerals.)
On the restoration of the clavicle when it is fractured. (Note: "furcula" refers to the clavicle bone.)
For some time, the clavicle is broken at the front edge and this fracture can occur in one of three ways. (Note: "manieyra" refers to a way or manner.)
Because it is broken (fractured), separated, or separated into two parts other than osteitis (inflammation of the bone); and it is easy to restore; or because it occurs with osteitis, and restoration is difficult; or because the fracture is associated with a plague.
And the operation in that, when the fracture is without wound, is that you make two ministers present, so that one holds the assistance and follows the broken neck, and the other extends the neck to that parted side.
Then align the fracture with the teeth that the form may be made according to its shape, and it should not be in a hollow or concave position.
If more extension is needed, then it is necessary that you place a support of fabric or wool over the patient's chest, and its size should be according to your need; stretch, lift and compress the neck and press the chest with your hand toward the fracture until it is aligned as required.
If you cannot pull the fracture's end out through the partition outside, which is necessary for the traction, then it is necessary that the patient is supine on their neck, and place a large size thumb or hand on one of the muscles.
And the servant presses the muscle of it on the outside of the fracture until the bone of the neck is lifted to the traction point above, and then you correct the fracture and align it with the teeth.
If symptoms of the neck (pain) and swelling are felt, it is necessary that you cut through it and extract those symptoms with ease.
If it is retained in the bone, you make an incision through it with one of the incisors that you have prepared for this purpose, as you had placed the saw on the neck that serves for the chisel; and it is a chisel made of wood or iron, and its shape is like:
And it is similar to the shape of a chisel, with its concavity facing downward; and let its width be according to your need, depending on the size of the bone or the hollowness of it.
But the length of it should be according to what is possible in the operation; that it has two ends like this, and one end should be wide and the other narrow.
If the wound caused by the incision that you made when extracting the swelling of the bone is wide and certain of pus, then you adjust the two lips of the wound with suture.
If the wound is small or you fear pus, then you fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the size of the wound's incision.
If pus occurs, then you pour wine vinegar and rose oil over it.
And in addition, the stick should not be harmful to the fractured bone; and therefore, you must examine the patient every day, as when the swelling is reduced or the stick is removed, then you correct it and tighten the bandage.
And place the patient's head on a pillow or bolster and sleep with their head slightly elevated, so that the support under their neck is also elevated, which raises the fracture of the neck to an elevation of the muscle; or it may be that their arms are placed on their neck.
And do not remove the bandage unless pus or pressure sores occur in the area before twelve days.
And renew the plaster if you see any signs of infection in the area, and adjust it in the bandage and restore it until it is restored and the fracture of the neck is coagulated.
And it should be strengthened and comforted according to the usual period of twenty-eight days, and in some cases in men, it is done within the month's time of that.
Chapter VI.
On the restoration of shoulder fracture.
The spatula is broken at its middle part and not completely, but only at the edges.
Therefore when it is broken in its middle part, and not completely, but only at the edges, it is recognized by touch alone. Then according to the shape of the fracture, prepare for its reduction and align it in accordance with its natural position in any possible way that you can.
Place powdered poppy seeds mixed with white of egg and stopper lion; place double-fold cloth over it; place on the steel rod of thin table according to the size of the spatula or a little more.
But if the rod is concave at one of the parts of the spatula, then you fill that concavity with stopper lion and adjust the rod accordingly.
Place strong and long human hair over it, with tight constriction and secure the rod inside so that it does not protrude from its place.
Consider the bandage every day; and every time it becomes soft, replace it with a new one and tighten and adjust the rod if it protrudes from its place.
Wash the patient over his own bed.
The spatula will heal in 20 days or a little more.
Therefore when this number is completed, you should remove the bandage and be sure that the bones from which it began have healed and their dissolution is not feared.
And if there is pressure on the bone and you see swelling, then cut it open and remove it, and proceed as previously mentioned in the cervical fracture treatment or cauterize the abscess if an abscess occurs here.
Chapter VII.
On the restoration of the fracture of the foot.
The foot is sometimes broken in its middle part and it heals by itself; but the ends move which are swollen and crushed.
The signs that the middle part is broken are that it inclines towards the sides and a great pain occurs, difficulty in breathing, coughing, high fever and vomiting; and it is visible in the cavity of the broken bone and not hidden from sensation.
The treatment of this is that the patient lies on his back, a cushion placed between his shoulder blades; and press the muscles of it, adjust the ribs of it with both hands and be careful during the operation and in the alignment according to any possible method that is appropriate and convenient, until the shape of the bone returns as it should.
Place a plaster and stopper on it, and place a thin steel rod of willow or walnut and its equivalent in weight, then wrap it; be careful not to damage the broken bone; move it slowly according to the curvature of the back for some time, and tighten it with proper constriction; consider the position at all times, and when it becomes soft, tighten it again.
And if necessary, you should cause its removal due to corrosion or prussic acid, pain, or abscess that occurs in the area; then cut and release it, remove the plaster and correct any problems caused by it with methods of healing it; and replace the plaster as needed; and adjust its tightness until it is healed.
Chapter VIII.
On the restoration of the ribs when they are broken.
Know that the fracture of the ribs occurs in large parts and not near the spine; and at the ends of these divisions, it does not occur due to compression but because they are cartilaginous. And the detection of this is not hidden from sensation, so you should investigate it with questions.
And the treatment is to fill the fracture with questions according to any possible method until it is filled as necessary, and then apply a plaster and tighten the broken bone with a rod, if necessary.
And if the fracture of the ribs is declining towards the sides, then the patient experiences intense pain and puncture, like the pain that occurs in those who have pleurisy, and for this reason the lining of the lung should be applied; and there will be difficulty breathing, coughing, and vomiting with blood; and this is difficult to heal.
The ancients were skilled in that area with many ingenious methods.
And they said: it is necessary to stop the food of the patient that produces inflammation and wind, so that his stomach may be inflated and extended, and the fracture should be pushed towards the sides outside.
But we should avoid this as much as possible to prevent the formation of abscess, if not necessary; and if it occurs, help with it immediately.
Some of them said it should be stopped above the bladder and that urine should be expelled; this is like reasoning, but it is feared that the bladder may attract impurities to the area due to its weakness.
Some of them said: it should be covered with wool that has been immersed in hot oil, and powder should be placed between the ribs until it is filled, and the position should be aligned when wrapping it according to its curvature; and the patient should be treated with pleurisy diet and medicine.
And if the patient is experiencing intense constriction which they cannot bear, and their bones press on a tender spot through puncture, fear for the patient arises. Therefore, it is necessary to make an incision at that location, expose the fractured rib, cover it with the instrument mentioned before (which preserves the opening), remember its recall, and cut the bone easily, then extract it.
And then we adjust the two lips of the wound (if it is large), sew them up with sutures, and care for it with plasters until it heals.
If there is an abscess inside (a collection of pus), then you must make an incision and pour warm oil on the affected area, wait for it to heal, and take care of the patient with whatever is required for an internal abscess.
The person should lie down on their side on the affected side until they recover.
Chapter IX.
On the restoration of the fracture of the spine and neck.
Some fractures occur in the bones of the neck and soon thereafter cause their adhesion, similar to fractures of the spine in the back. And if you want to know whether a fracture will heal or not, consider that if the muscles around it are already softened and stiffened and dead and cannot be strengthened or extended, and when you press on them with your fingers or prick them with a needle and do not feel pain in them, then you know that it is not healing, and in this case, it is fatal within a month.
And if the muscles are bad and feel constriction when pressed or pricked with a needle, then you know that the neck of the bone is saved, and the patient will recover with treatment.
If you see a fracture of the spine in the neck and want to know if it will heal or not, consider the position of the legs (paralysis).
If you see that they are already softened (meaning the fracture has healed), then apply whatever is appropriate for such cases, and when they lie on their back, they vomit involuntarily and have a feeling of restlessness and sometimes lie on their chest and cannot urinate, then you know it is fatal, and do not bother with its treatment.
But if none of these occur, the cause is better and easier to manage.
The treatment for this is to perform the incision of the abscess with a warm cautery applied to the adhered spine, or with mullein leaves soaked in oil; apply it several times a day until the abscess is healed.
Then apply a comforting and healing plaster, strengthen it with lint, and command the patient to rest and be calm and not sleep on the affected side until it heals.
And if it is necessary to adhere the bone to the cartilage or any cause that has been separated from it, then you must dry the affected area and remove the bone; then adjust the two lips of the wound (if it is large), sew them up with sutures; and cover it with a dressing made of ointment until it heals.
But if the joint of the bone at the ankle is broken and the end of the tendon is affected, then you must insert your left hand into the buttocks, grab the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible by equalizing it, and cover it with a plaster and splint if necessary, and strengthen it.
If you feel broken joints in it (the patient), then cut them open, remove the bone, and treat the wound with the treatment prescribed until it heals.
Chapter X.
On the restoration of the inguinal hernia.
The bones of the inguinal area are usually broken, and if they break, their fracture is not a true fracture but rather a dislocation at the extremity that is pushed inwards and goes through the length and slope towards the affected side; and when the patient feels pain in that area and pressure or pricking with a needle, the hernia swells when it is broken.
The restoration of this is to stretch your hand over it until it covers the fracture, which is the shape of the hernia.
If the fracture is only at the end of it (the hernia), then you grab it as much as possible according to what is appropriate for equalizing it, until it resembles its natural shape.
But if the fracture is in the length or slopes towards the affected side, then you have the patient lie on their stomach until the restoration of the fracture is prepared. And when you have grabbed it, apply a plaster to it, and cover it with a wooden or cloth splint, and strengthen it by tying it tightly, without fear of causing further fracture or removal of the splint.
Grab the concavity with what fills it until it tightens evenly, and command the patient to sleep on their back or on their healthy side.
But if an abscess forms (a collection of pus), you stop the extension and restoration of it until the abscess is healed, and cover it with whatever is appropriate to heal it; then return to the restoration of the hernia, and strengthen it as necessary.
And if this happens in the bones at the joints or they are fractured, you treat them.
Title. XI.
On the Restoration of Adjuvant Bone Fractures.
The adjutor is that which is between the shoulder and the neck.
And if it is broken, the restoration of it is done in one of two ways, of which one is that you take a flat piece of lead of medium thickness according to this form:
And attach them at both ends with straps, and place it in a raised location.
Follow the patient over a seat, and throw his broken arm into the hole up to the handle, which is in the middle of where the curve of the pole is attached.
And suspend it for some serious reason, or the minister extends it to the outside; and have the doctor and his assistants near where the fracture is, until the fracture returns as needed.
And the other method is that the patient lies on his back and suspends his arm from his neck with a strap, and commands two assistants to one of them to hold what is above the fracture with their hands; and the other to hold the pillow below him; and each one extends to their side.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, you must pull on the fracture with a strap and on the other side with another strap, and each of the two assistants extends to their sides.
And therefore if the fracture is close to the muscle's edge, it is necessary that half of the strap be placed on the handle and the other half on the side of the fracture.
Similarly, if the fracture is near the joint, it is necessary that the strap be placed at that location and on that joint.
Tie it with ease and without effort, and apply it as necessary and in good alignment, then pull on it if an abscess does not develop.
But if an abscess forms, leave the tightening for seven days and apply wool soaked in warm vinegar and rose oil over it until the abscess subsides, then pull on it.
And the method of strengthening it is to apply plaster on the fracture, and place a cover of thick cloth over the plaster; and adjust the arm on that support, and place his hand open on the muscle.
And hold the cloth and pull on the support for the arm to be in a stable position, unless there is any reason that prevents it due to the broken bone.
And you use splints.
And you place a wider and stronger splint on the fracture than the others; and between each splint leave the width of a finger, and the length of the splints should be according to the fracture with an addition of three digits in total.
And pull on the splints with the tightening mentioned at the beginning of this chapter.
And your tightening on the site of the fracture should be tighter. And as the fracture becomes longer, the tightening should become smaller.
And therefore if the situation of the splints and the tightening is as described at the time when you restore the limb to its position, then do it.
And if you fear an abscess, leave the tightening and splints for seven days as described.
And consider the strap in all parts for three days so that there is no immediate or inflammation at the site, or food is prohibited from reaching the limb due to excessive tightening of the strap; and therefore correct everything as described.
But if you are certain that there will be no problem with this, do not remove the ligature until at least three days or more.
The patient's sleep should be on his back, and his hands on his stomach.
And place a hot cooked splint full of woolen bandage on the injured limb, and consider at all times of the night or day that the shape of the broken limb should not be loosened or dissolved, or that the straps are softened; therefore correct everything with your judgment.
The patient's food should be according to the order that was previously mentioned, in such a way that the food is subtle at first until it becomes stronger; and therefore you must make it coarse.
In custom of the assistant and bedside, they are strengthened for forty days; and then it is necessary that they be discontinued and a bath given, and medicine administered with oil, which corrects [the condition].
And if the fracture is open, then you do not remove the ligament and splint immediately, but leave it for days or up to two months.
12th Chapter
On the restoration of the fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the two focils; one is small, and it is the one that is referred to as the little head, and the other is large and is the one that is placed below the small one, from the lower part of the arm.
And when only the large bone (focal major) is broken or both are fractured together.
And when the small bone (focal minor) is broken above, the restoration of it is easy, and its healing is more easy.
And when the bone below (the larger one) is broken, the fracture of it is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And even worse if both bones are fractured together.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the small one above, it is appropriate for the doctor to make a small extension with ease before the wound is sewn up.
But if the large bone is fractured, a more forceful extension is necessary.
And if both bones are fractured together, you must make the extraction less forcefully.
You must make the figure of the hand with the restoration and extension stretched out on a cushion, and place the fingers at the edges above, as all others have said, and the ear (auricular) should be lower than the others, and the patient should lie down centred over a low couch.
And place a cushion in front of him at his height or inclined to him, so that the patient does not suffer labor.
And bring the minister (assistant) to stretch out the arm from above to below with his hand or a ligament, and another minister should do the same above.
And bring the doctor to sew up the bone inside so that he reduces it according to the best shape possible.
And if there is a fissure in the bone, you must carefully reduce all things in their place with your skill.
And if a fissure appears separated that pierces the skin and is not hopeful for restoration of it, then you make an incision over it and remove it according to the manner we have described above.
But if the fracture is with an ulcer, then refer to the special chapter we have already discussed, and thus take care of it.
And if an abscess arises at the beginning of your restoration, then you should line a cloth with a suture made of rose oil and white wax, which is moderate in thickness and strength, and stretch the cloth over it with a subtle string until the abscess is softened; then remove the suture and apply an emplaster prepared from milled powder with clay of huou, and place splints; and let the splint that is placed over the fracture be firm and strong.
And you should know that the number of splints for the arm is seven in the major positions, or if the fracture is in one bone or two bones together.
And place your string over the location of the fracture firmly and forcefully, and when the extraction is to the edges above or below the fracture, place the string a little less tight according to the reminder given at the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture should be soft and moist, not hard or harsh, and the thread with which it is strengthened should be of moderate thickness, as we have recommended above.
Consider the limb (arm) and the ligament for several days. And if any cause arises for which correction is necessary, such as a swelling on the limb, then you must bind the limb with warm water until it ceases.
Unbind the limb and let it rest until it is healed, and then replace the string.
And if the extraction has been softened and the bone has healed and appears similar to others, then correct everything with your skill.
Consider more if the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excess binding, for then it is necessary to soften it slightly and leave it for several days until the nourishment reaches it, and strengthen it.
But if there is no such cause for the patient as we have mentioned, then it is not necessary to remove it before about twenty days or more after that.
And let the hand of the patient be suspended by its ability to move agitatedly, and place it to sleep on its own back.
You know that this fracture is restored in 30 days or 40 days, or it heals quickly in 28 days, and all of this depends on the complexity of the case and the properties of the individual.
Chapter XIII.
On the healing of a finger fracture and thumb fracture.
You know that a finger fracture or thumb sprain can lead to a fracture, but it does not occur unless excessive movement is observed.
When the finger or thumb is fractured or sprained, it is necessary that the patient remains immobilized and elevated at an angle equal to the elbow, and place a pad under the affected area.
Then the assistant should extend the fractured bones, and the doctor will align them properly using good alignment.
It is necessary to apply a plaster and padding, unless an abscess occurs; place a steel splint as needed over the affected area, and you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
However, if the fracture is in the palm area, you should place a bandage around it and instruct the patient to grasp the broken part with their other hand. The bandage should be tightened with a long strip of cloth.
The splint should be made of wood or some other material that allows for elevation, so that the palm is in contact with it and the splint should be tightened as necessary.
If the fracture is in the fingers outside of the palm, you should place a splint above and another below the finger on the palm side to open and straighten the hand. Then apply a tight bandage as needed, like wrapping the hand around the wrist using linen strips.
If a toe or knuckle of the finger is fractured, if possible, immerse it in warm water and align it as necessary and tighten with a small straight splint to raise the fracture and prevent it from worsening.
If the fracture is in the other fingers, such as in the middle finger or index finger or thumb or ring finger, immerse it in warm water and align it with the affected digit or all of them together if possible, which is better; or place a small splint over it as mentioned earlier for the toe.
Consider in your treatment the disposal of an abscess and its prevention with proper operations, as well as what should be done when any cause of this occurs, according to the narrative provided.
Chapter XIV.
On the healing of a hip bone fracture.
A fractured hip bone is recognized by the feeling in the affected area and swelling above and below the groin.
The healing process involves wrapping a bandage around the fracture and another below it, and the patient lies on their back with their legs raised.
The assistant should extend the bandage over the entire affected area when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
If the dislocation of the hip joint is suspected, apply a bandage to the lower area around the pubic bone, so that the extension covers above, and another bandage below the fracture.
Similarly, if the fracture is suspected to be in the lower area, apply a bandage near the pubic bone and let the extension reach the areas below.
The doctor should align the bone with both hands inside and reduce it as naturally as possible so that the bone is properly aligned and joined well by good joining; then apply a plaster and bandage, unless an abscess occurs in the affected limb. If an abscess occurs, leave it for several days until the abscess has been treated, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be applied like this: wrap a firm bandage around the fracture, fold it over two or three times, and remove any excess; then wrap a cloth around the affected limb up to the ankle and hip joint.
Insert a long probe between the thigh and hip close to the pelvis at the dividing point, and let the ends of the probe extend from the lower division more than from both parts, then roll it back and forth over the thigh and hip so that the excess of the bandage is removed.
Then pause on the thigh at the location of that fracture, and place one steel rod on the bone of the hip.
Then fill the empty spaces between thigh and pelvis with much cloth, to coagulate the extrusion.
Begin with a strong bandage around the middle at the location of the fracture with three or four wrappings, and as you get further from the location of the fracture, your bandage should be lighter and less wet.
Extend the ends of the probe to the dividing points on both sides of the foot until it reaches the heel of the foot, and strengthen the ends of the rods from the other side so that the bandage does not slip out of its place.
Lay the bandage on that which does not cause corrosion of the member, nor cauterization abscess or swelling and resemble those; and if any such occurrence arises, then immediately remove it, and correct whatever occurred in an appropriate manner, as I have said many times before.
But if there is pain in the bone that you have set, then it is necessary for you to immobilize it as much as possible; and if you cannot do so, fumigate it and remove it, and heal it with what follows the memory of the cure until it heals.
This fracture of the thigh is strengthened more than those that are set with rods, as I have said in the assistant and arm.
But in the treatment of this fracture in such a manner does not cause the patient's limping; and if it is treated alone, another thigh must be applied to it, then it is necessary for the one affected to always limp.
Know that the thigh is strengthened in 40 days or a few months or a few weeks, and all of this depends on the diversity of complications and other circumstances.
Chapter XV.
On the treatment of the fracture of the heel bone.
Know that the fracture of the heel bone heals more slowly than in many observed cases.
But if there is a fracture, then it is only a fissure or crack in its divisions, and that is with one or two plagues, and all of this is with the sensation.
The treatment of it is such that what is broken in its divisions should be immobilized until it is united and consolidated as much as possible by means of easy, simple, and convenient materials.
Then apply plaster and place a round rod on it if necessary; and strengthen above with bandage as appropriate.
And consider all the dispositions that we have recommended in other fractures, such as cauterization abscess and similar to these as long as they oppose any accident that is appropriate for it until it heals.
Chapter XVI.
On the treatment of a hip fracture.
Know that in the hip there are two bones of which one is large and is called the pelvis; and the other is thin, and is called the acetabulum.
And if there are any of the species of fracture that occur in the two bones of the arm, and its treatment is done as in the treatment of the arm by immobilization, and the operation is one.
And if the bones are crushed together, the pelvis is converted into all divisions.
And if the thin bone is crushed, the pelvis is converted to the anterior divisions.
And if the large bone is crushed, move it backwards, and it is due to those causes that you are not familiar with.
And apply extension, alignment, and ligature of the rods according to the immobilization.
But if the pelvis fracture is shallow and has many cavities, then the extension should be less and lighter, and alleviate it with your skill.
In the pelvis is any cause for operation on the arm, and it occurs when you immobilize the rods and complete all the necessary procedures, then you will take pieces of pine wood that are placed under the causes that are already cushioned with soles, and they are made in exactly the same way as branches of palm; alleviate those with a small amount of coarseness, and it should not be too thick, and the length of these should correspond to the length of the pelvis from the hip to the heel.
Then cover each one with two layers of cloth according to its length, and place one on the dividing line of the pelvis of one side and the other on the other side, and they should be from the hip to the heel of the foot.
And place the two rods in three locations of both ends and in the middle location, because with this bandage the pelvis is prevented from clinking on the left and right sides, and it is a good position.
And apply a splint of wood according to the length of the pelvis; it should be placed on the pelvis to prevent movement.
And moreover, you must do this for a fracture, and when it is indeed an open wound.
And daily observe the wound and study it further, and keep it from abscess and inflammation or other similar causes; and when any of those occur, then apply what is necessary until it heals.
Chapter 17.
On the fracture of the heel bone and the bones of the foot, and on the toes.
Not every part of the heel bone suffers a fracture; instead, certain bones in the foot may experience fractures; and the toes of the foot rarely experience fractures, except for strains during the months of discomfort.
And if the bones of the foot undergo a fracture and the bone fragments are elevated above their original position, then the patient should place his/her foot on a prepared surface such as when walking.
The patient should place one foot on the elevated bone and apply pressure, push, and hold it in place until it returns to its original position.
The patient should place a plaster and a pad on top of it, and under the foot, place a small table with two hollow spaces.
And bind that part of the foot in a downward position with bandages, as you have already wrapped it using various methods.
And after three or four days, you should remove the bandages because you will observe the bones that are held in place, either broken or dislocated, and the operation in such cases is as follows.
But if the toes are broken, then you should set them, and hold them in place as I have previously recommended for the restoration of the hand's fingers. And place the broken toe in the position it was before, slightly wider than its original width; and under the foot, place the table that I have recommended to you, and bind it with appropriate bandages.
But if the fracture involves more than two or three bones, then you should place a splint on each toe of its appropriate size wrapped in linen cloth, and secure the table under the heel of the foot, with it standing on one of the table's legs to keep it stable.
And be careful to remember that you should not overlook all the causes mentioned in previous discussions on fractures of the various accidents that have been described; and always apply remedies for any such occurrence.
Chapter 18.
On the fracture of the coccyx in women, the pelvis bone, and the sacrum in men.
When the coccyx of a woman is broken, you should cause it to heal in a fallen position, and incline it slightly towards the posterior part, and hold it behind.
The midwife should pack the coccyx with cotton, and apply it in the same way as usual; and the woman should be shaken, and gradually raise her hips slightly, for everything will heal behind the coccyx until it is in front of the split bone, and the broken bone returns to its original position.

And fill it with cotton and keep it as I have recommended until it heals.

Title (or Chapter) XXIII
On speech in dislocation.
Dislocation is simply one of the joints of the body that cannot move due to its dislocation, and it distorts the shape of the limb, causing pain and severe injuries on the patient.
Therefore, when an event occurs with a dislocation, it is necessary to perform reduction immediately, and not to delay its reduction, because if delayed, the limb becomes necrotic, and reduction of dislocation becomes difficult.
And for this reason, it is necessary that the limb be neither numb nor stretched at the time of necrosis, because many times it causes spasms and painful swelling.
But when this happens, it is necessary that you apply cautery to the patient, and quickly stop the necrosis; and cover the limb with warm water and oil, and reduce it easily.
And treat all parts with the remedy that will return in its place.
And you have already arranged the chapters on dislocation as previously described in fractures; from the upper to lower parts of the body.
Title (or Chapter) XXIV.
On treatment of mandible dislocation below.
If both mandibles are dislocated, it should not be prolonged; and the dislocation of both is made in one of two ways: either they remain stuck in their places due to little movement, and are slightly mollified; or they are completely dislocated, so that they must be mollified at the junction until saliva from the patient's mouth flows out and he cannot take it or close his mouth, and his tongue is wrapped in his cheek.
But if the dislocation is slight, then it returns by itself with minimal intervention.
If the dislocation is complete and prolonged, it is necessary to perform a reduction of it gently, without delay.

And it should be given gently, and avoid forcing the opening of the mouth, with regard to food, drink and speech, until the mandible is mollified and healed.

Chapter XXVI

Chapter 30.
On the treatment of dislocation of the vertebrae in the spine.

Chapter .xxx1.
On the treatment of a dislocated pelvis.
Know that the joint of the pelvis and the joint of the muscle do not dislocate but only move, and this does not occur in those who suffer from other dislocations due to minor displacement and concavity.
And the joint of the pelvis can be dislocated in four ways: it is dislocated inside at the dividing lines, or outside at the dividing lines, or in front at the dividing lines, or behind at the dividing lines.
Moreover, it is more often dislocated internally, but less so in front or behind.
The sign of the internal dislocation is that when you align the healthy and diseased thighs together, the healthy one appears longer and the affected one shorter, and the patient cannot bend his knee; this area should be cauterized with a small cautery to prevent further infection since the tip of the abscess has already been reached.
The sign of external dislocation is that the symptoms of the affected area are opposite to those we have described.
The sign of anterior dislocation is that the thigh expands according to its nature, but does not bend without pain in the front, and if it is perforated and cannot be moved forward; urine is retained and the abscess is drained, and walking should be done on the heel.
The displacement of that person who experiences dislocation at the rear parts is indicated by the fact that it does not expand the anus, nor can it bend the anterior part that bends the posterior one, and the leg is shorter than the other, and the dislocation is softened, and the head of the dislocated joint is to be placed in that location.
The reduction of the type of this dislocation that you are considering is as follows: if the dislocation is old and long-standing, which has already become ossified, then you administer the reduction of it and leave it in its position, but this is not the entire cure; therefore, it is not necessary to perform an operation on it.
However, in the case of a recent dislocation, which is one of the four types of dislocations, you should try to reposition the joint and extend it between the thighs both inside and outside, on the right and left, forcibly returning it and not requiring any other treatment.
However, if it does not return in this manner, you must then prepare and administer strong medicine, extending the leg of the affected thigh farther down or holding it with a ligament that is attached above the anus; and another person should extend the hand of the dislocated limb upwards to place its other end lower on the thighs; and another person should apply strong pressure with a rope on the side of the dislocated joint, and another person should hold taut the end of the rope.
The extension of the leg or thigh between the anus to the front of the neck, or from the back thigh to the chest.
The extension should be made in one move so that the patient is raised with their own body from the ground and remains hanging.
This type of extension is common to all four types.
And if the dislocation returns with the method described earlier, it is good; but if not, it is necessary for all types of cases to follow the method I have described for treatment.
In the reduction of the dislocation, if it is in the thighs, it is necessary that the patient lies on their side; and place the ligaments on the side of the dislocated joint at the location between the head of the joint and the point where the ligament is to be placed; and extend the ligament above the part of the anus to the frontmost part of the neck.
And another strong person should grab the arms of the dislocated limb, and press hard on the thick part of the patient's dislocated joint, and extend it outwardly by forceful extension, as it will return to its place.
This type of reduction is easier to cure than other types for this affected limb.
However, if the patient is excused and does not agree to this type of treatment for all cases, you must then bind both feet of the patient with a strong, light ligament, around both ankles and both buttocks.
Let the length of each one be the number of four fingers, and let the affected leg be extended more than the other by a distance of two fingers.
And let the patient be suspended from the top of the post and hang down as far as possible; and let the length of the rope from the ground be two arms.
And command a strong young man to push the head of the joint, and let another strong young man be suspended with the patient, and the third strong young man should pull on the joint forcefully, as the joint will return to its place quickly.
However, in the reduction of the dislocation if it is outside the thigh, you must let the patient walk on crutches as I have recommended earlier; and apply strong pressure to the affected leg itself and its feet.
And let two posts be placed, one at the feet of the patient, and the other at the head; and let two ropes be tied to the middle of each post that are adjusted so that drapes with wet cloth are wrapped around them, so that the patient is not harmed by the post being between their thighs, for you must ensure that the extension to the backside is not narrow.
And let each person extend their part, and the doctor with his hand guides the reduction according to art (medical practice).
And if the dislocation is in front of the thighs, you must gently extend the leg as described earlier on the pole; and the doctor places his right palm on the affected leg, and another person compresses it with the other hand, and with that compression, the person extends the leg to the backside to the anus.
And if the dislocation is in back of the thighs, it is not necessary to extend the leg outwards, and the patient should be elevated above the ground; you must lie down on a hard surface, as it is necessary to do so for the dislocation of the hips as I have said earlier, with the patient lying between two hard surfaces; and the face and the ligaments should be extended, as described earlier, without any improvement.
It is necessary to administer a compression on a table at the location of the joint.
In order to perform the reduction, you must first extend two blankets of the patient and when you see that the intestine has relaxed and the patient opens their blanket and expands it without excuse, then you know which member is being reduced — and then you adjust two cushions and apply a paste with a bandage so that it is not too wide in any of the parts; and keep the patient resting for three or four days.
Then unbind and remove the bandage, compare one blanket to the other.
If you see that the intestine is in its proper place, then you know that the dislocation has been closed, and let the patient rest.
If you see any cause of swelling, then return and apply a poultice to it, and let it rest according to your first structure, and leave it for three days.
Then unbind that one, and have him walk slowly on each side for several days, until he is comfortable.
Chapter XXXII.
Treatise on the treatment of dislocation of the ankle.
The ankle can be dislocated in three ways: either at the outside parts, the inside parts, the front parts (more so than the back parts); but it is not dislocated at the front parts.
The sign of ankle dislocation is when you ask the patient to apply their blanket to the affected limb, and then you know that the ankle is dislocated.
The restoration of all the ways of ankle dislocation is to place the patient correctly, so that they extend their blanket if possible; hold your hands on the sides of it, and keep their foot between your feet; and bring the ankle towards you with your palms, and hold it there until it returns to its proper location, and another servant extends their foot until the ankle is back in place.
The sign of reduction of the ankle is when the blanket adheres to the affected limb without any effort.
Then apply a poultice to it and have the blanket adhere to the affected limb, and bind it for three or four days, then unbind it.
Do not let the patient walk for several days until he is healed and comfortable.
But if the reduction of the ankle is not possible for you, then administer strong traction with bandages as described in the treatment of the hip.
Chapter XXXIII.
Treatise on the treatment of dislocation of the ankle.
The ankle may occasionally be dislocated by a slight movement, and occasionally by a blow, and the dislocation of the ankle can occur on the inside parts or the outside parts.
The sign of dislocation is that sometimes the thigh appears swollen, leading to separation at the point where it is dislocated.
The cure for dislocation is easy reduction and that it should be extended with ease and guided back into place.
In the treatment of a dislocated joint following the principle of complement, it is necessary to immobilize the patient and have a strong and skilled assistant from behind the hip in the same location.
Then place your right hand on the top of the foot and your left hand on the lower part of the leg in the hip area (pelvis).
Then pull the foot toward you with your right hand, pull it twice more with your left hand, extend it with your left hand and push the leg's thigh against the bed with your right hand.
Do this repeatedly as previously mentioned.
Then push the leg's thigh against the bed on the third pull and you are in the pelvis area.
If it returns in one or two attempts using this method and if the foot is guided back, that's good; otherwise, continue with the operation since it will return again.
If reduction of the joint is prohibited by the patient's condition as previously recommended, then place the patient on their back on the ground, spread out a flat stone on the firm ground and have the patient lie down with their feet in the box, and you have already wrapped them in cloth to prevent injury to the patient.
The assistant should hold the patient's feet and another assistant should extend the foot with their hands or use ropes to keep it on top of the neck; each assistant should then extend that limb in the same direction, and the stone should remain between the patient's legs to prevent the body from being pulled apart at the joints.
The doctor should guide the dislocation back with their hands and another assistant should hold the healthy limb at the joints since the dislocation returns easily.
Therefore, when the dislocation returns and is declared as the reduction of the joint, you should stop applying plaster and pull it off firmly, and tie the foot to the bed.
You must be careful or protect the nerves that are behind the thigh to ensure that the ropes around it are not tight and do not injure it.
Then leave the patient for two or three days; if the ropes become soaked, change them.
Then release the patient on the third or fourth day, and prohibit the patient from moving for forty days. If the patient is forced to move in that space, it is not guaranteed that the dislocation will not be dissolved and damaged, and that it will not receive treatment.
If an abscess forms due to cauterization, you must administer the treatment mentioned in our narrative in another location for healing and dressing until it heals.
Chapter XXXIV.
On the treatment of dislocation of the toes.
It is necessary that the dislocated joint be guided back gently without force; this should not be difficult and can be easily done.
If the dislocation is in one of the toes on the hip, it is necessary to immobilize the patient's foot on a flat surface or table and keep them as if they were walking.
Stand on one foot, and place the other foot on an object that supports the joints, and press down with your other foot until it returns, ensuring it is guided back properly without appearing in a prominent position.
Place the pelvis of the foot on a table that supports the entire foot with two legs, and tighten it securely with a suitable structure for three days; then release it and allow it to rest from walking for several days until it is strengthened and the reduction is assured.
Chapter XXXV.
On the types of dislocation that are caused by injury or fracture, or any combination thereof.
When such a cause occurs and you proceed with the treatment of that injury and restoration, many times death ensues.
Therefore, in the treatment of that injury, one must not presume but be knowledgeable and experienced in the art, long practice, mercy, compassion, patience, prudence, and humility; use only cauterizing medicines at first, leave the patient to divine judgment, but help those who hope for salvation from perdition with relief from the illness, and where there is any hope. And then forcefully perform the reduction at the beginning of the cause, before an abscess appears cauterized.
If the limb returns as desired, then administer the regimen which will cause the abscess to subside and heal the wound with whatever is appropriate for dry soothing agents.
If a dislocation is accompanied by a fracture and separated fragments, then you perform the treatment for them, and do what we have said in simple fractures, but before that in their locations.
Be careful and avoid as much as possible the instrument within the fearful paths according to your ability, for that is better for you to obey your Creator, and more enduring to your glory, and more healthy to your true body in your times. In this way, the words of Al-Zahrawi are completed perfectly, may God be praised. Amen.
